  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer618797169627749289">It is 1919, and Niska, the last Oji-Cree woman to live off the land, has received word that one of the two boys she saw off to the Great War has returned. Xavier Bird, her sole living relation, is gravely wounded and addicted to morphine. As Niska slowly paddles her canoe on the three-day journey to bring Xavier home, travelling through the stark but stunning landscape of </span>
  <span id="freeText618797169627749289" style="display:none">It is 1919, and Niska, the last Oji-Cree woman to live off the land, has received word that one of the two boys she saw off to the Great War has returned. Xavier Bird, her sole living relation, is gravely wounded and addicted to morphine. As Niska slowly paddles her canoe on the three-day journey to bring Xavier home, travelling through the stark but stunning landscape of Northern Ontario, their respective stories emerge—stories of Niska’s life among her kin and of Xavier’s horrifying experiences in the killing fields of Ypres and the Somme.</span>

Popular Answered Questions

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/950993-i-got-chatting-to-someone-at-a-starbucks">I got chatting to someone at a Starbucks the other day who was reading this book to see whether it was appropriate for students studying First Nations people. As an Australian who has recently arrived in Victoria, I&#39;m really interested in the difference between Canada and Australia&#39;s treatment, and acceptance, of First Nations people. Do people find the book to be balanced? respectful? All opinions are welcome!</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_950993'><a id="like_count_community_question_950993" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/950993?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F823411.Three_Day_Road">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/950993-i-got-chatting-to-someone-at-a-starbucks">3 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/950993-i-got-chatting-to-someone-at-a-starbucks">See all 2 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/10715801-m-ch-l-n"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1536519764i/10715801._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/10715801-m-ch-l-n">Míchílín</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I am interested in this same topic from a Canadian perspective. I wouldn't recommend Boyden at all to satiate this curiosity as he has recently been o<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I am interested in this same topic from a Canadian perspective. I wouldn't recommend Boyden at all to satiate this curiosity as he has recently been outed as an imposter. Of course, anyone with an understanding of First Nations literature on Turtle Island would have been able to tell just by reading Boyden's books that he doesn't really have any First Nations heritage -- he writes very much in the tradition of CanLit where conflict with nature is a major theme in contrast to FN authors for whom connection to the land is almost always a central theme. Boyden is not an authentic voice in the project of #Reconciliation.<br /><br />If you're looking for First Nations authors describing the Canadian context, I recommend Richard Wagamese first and foremost as he manages to tell the painful truth about colonialism in Canada while keeping you rolling on the floor with laughter. _Keeper'n Me_ and _Indian Horse_ should be required reading in all high schools in Canada. Thomas King is another great FN storyteller with much literary merit and conscious things to say. I would start with these two.<br /><br />It's also interesting to contrast with the US experience as the British learned from watching the bloody "Indian Wars" in the US that the deceit of treaties would be the path of least resistance to obtaining land on the portion of Turtle Island now known as Canada. Also, many nations were artificially divided by the US-Canadian border and that is an ongoing issue on both sides of it today. There was a time, for example, where a Mohawk woman from Ontario would lose her Indian Status if she married a Mohawk man from New York. Two of my favourite FN authors writing in the US side of Turtle Island are Sherman Alexie and Leslie Marmon Silko.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1429354488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10263175107852391004"><i>"My body hums with Nephew’s pain and with the realization that he has come home only to die."</i><br/><br/>Xavier Bird has returned to northern Ontario after living through the hell of the fighting and trenches of WWI. He is wounded, dispirited, and addicted to morphine. The one soul who still cares for him in this world will journey several days to meet him at the train station and bring him home. Niska, or ‘Auntie’, is the last of the Ojibwa-Cree awawatuks, those that have rejected the ways of the white man</span>
  <span id="freeText10263175107852391004" style="display:none"><i>"My body hums with Nephew’s pain and with the realization that he has come home only to die."</i><br /><br />Xavier Bird has returned to northern Ontario after living through the hell of the fighting and trenches of WWI. He is wounded, dispirited, and addicted to morphine. The one soul who still cares for him in this world will journey several days to meet him at the train station and bring him home. Niska, or ‘Auntie’, is the last of the Ojibwa-Cree awawatuks, those that have rejected the ways of the white man by living apart from the ways of civilization. She also has the power of visions, of divining, a gift that has been passed down to her from her father. It is a three day journey back down the river, and as Xavier’s supply of morphine dwindles away, Niska has little time to save this broken young man from the three day road and certain death. <i>"Their morphine eats men. It has fed on me for the last months, and when it is all gone I will be the one to starve to death. I will not be able to live without it."</i><br /><br />What struck me about this novel was that the Native American tradition of storytelling is very compelling here. Author Joseph Boyden weaves together the first-person narratives of both Niska and Xavier. Xavier’s story is unfolded through a series of flashbacks during his drug-induced state. Niska’s history is shared with us as she recounts to Xavier the stories of her childhood and of her life as an isolated woman. We learn of Xavier’s best friend, Elijah Whiskeyjack. Growing up together and learning from one another, these two later set out for the European front. Here the horror and violence of the war is brutally but honestly depicted. War will change a man. You are forced to make choices that will ensure your survival. But even in wartime, is there not a line that should not be crossed? Can one go too far? At what point does the instinct for survival turn to something worse perhaps… savagery, madness? Memories haunt and torment Xavier. A striking similarity is made between the killing fields of war with Niska’s stories of the windigo. A windigo is one that has turned to consuming human flesh in order to satisfy his or her hunger as a result of desperation followed by insanity. Can the absurdity of war be likened to the windigo? Niska shares a vision she once had:<br /><br /><i>"I knew even as a young woman that destruction bred on the horizon. In my early visions, numbers of men, higher than any of us could count, were cut down. They lived in the mud like rats and lived only to think of new ways to kill one another. No one is safe in such times, not even the Cree of Mushkegowuk. War touches everyone, and windigos spring from the earth."</i><br /><br />We are left to consider where that line exists between survival and madness, senselessness. Boyden will not necessarily give you the answers; instead he will leave you with much to ponder. I found this to be very powerfully written, with the strength being in the shared storytelling. If anything was lacking for me personally, it was in the writing style itself. The prose is fairly straightforward, which is fine and will suit many readers. I did not find myself reflecting on the beauty of the language in this book. Naturally, a war story depicting atrocities and what is worst in mankind may not necessarily be beautifully expressed. But it could be! Case in point – <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/355697.All_Quiet_on_the_Western_Front" title="All Quiet on the Western Front by Erich Maria Remarque" rel="nofollow noopener">All Quiet on the Western Front</a>. It is probably not fair to compare this book to that one, but having read the classic WW1 novel this past summer, I certainly could not help it. I use this only to illustrate a point, however, and would not at all hesitate to recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823411.Three_Day_Road" title="Three Day Road by Joseph Boyden" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Day Road</a>. In fact, I highly recommend it to anyone that is interested in learning about a piece of The Great War that is rarely covered in literature – that of the Canadian Native American contribution to that war. In his acknowledgements, the author indicates that this book was written to honor those Native American soldiers. In particular, he references what he considers one of the most important heroes of the time, <i>"Francis Pegahmagabow, sniper, scout, and later chief of Wasauksing First Nation."</i> This is not an easy book to read but I am very glad to have done so. From what I understand, this is Boyden’s debut novel, although he did publish a group of short stories prior to this. I can only imagine that his skill will shine even more in later works, and I can’t wait to read more of those on my list.<br /><br /><i>"I realized then that sadness was at the heart of the windigo, a sadness so pure that it shriveled the human heart and let something else grow in its place."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10263175107852391004" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30224040?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/916030-ruzz?shelf=2008">2008</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30224040" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2503357381225389007">I found Three Day Road when a notoriously late friend was more than an hour late to meet me and I had time to browse a local bookstore. I didn't pick the book up that day, but i noted it. <br/><br/>Later, while near the bookstore I went back in and grabbed it. The idea of the book crossed a number of vectors of interest for me. War history (wait, don't stop reading yet), snipers (please, keep reading), and early 20th century Natives. <br/><br/>I expected it to at least titillate my love of snipers, and war and the </span>
  <span id="freeText2503357381225389007" style="display:none">I found Three Day Road when a notoriously late friend was more than an hour late to meet me and I had time to browse a local bookstore. I didn't pick the book up that day, but i noted it. <br /><br />Later, while near the bookstore I went back in and grabbed it. The idea of the book crossed a number of vectors of interest for me. War history (wait, don't stop reading yet), snipers (please, keep reading), and early 20th century Natives. <br /><br />I expected it to at least titillate my love of snipers, and war and the romantic ideal of Canada's native people. <br /><br />What happened in fact is I was reminded that the medium of the novel is meant for many things, but of all those things, first is and always will be story-telling. <br /><br />There are always messages, and lessons, and commentary in all human thought, and likely all stories. But its so easy to become consumed by the desire to share an ideal, an idea, a view of the world--we lose sight of the greater need.<br /><br />That greater need is to be captivated, carried, led into another world entirely. To be entertained and touched. To be free of our reality and it's annoyances, stresses and even sometimes joys. And, in very rare cases, we need to find a safe place to feel more fully than the concrete world might permit. <br /><br />And it is through great story-telling we are reminded how we can love characters in books. Be stirred and impassioned by simple words. be felt fully human-confusingly-through our emotional range and capacity to feel. <br /><br />This book while not the benchmark upon which to measure storytelling, was still strong enough to remind me of this need. It's the first book I can recall in some time where I could turn off my analytical mind and just go with the characters. <br /><br />perhaps the last was A Fine Balance. <br /><br />Even if you care nothing for war history, snipers, or native idealism you should read this book for its great characters and ability to bring you somewhere else for 400 pages. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2503357381225389007" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1439725801" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1439725801" />
    <a title="Matthew Quann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22699228-matthew-quann"><img alt="Matthew Quann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528393487p2/22699228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1439725801?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Quann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Quann" href="/user/show/22699228-matthew-quann">Matthew Quann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143056959" href="/book/show/3011111-three-day-road">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=oh-canada">oh-canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1439725801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12886043618971387300">A novel that begins at the end, and ends with a beginning. <i>Three Day Road</i> is a stunning debut from one of Canada’s foremost writers, Joseph Boyden. When Xavier Bird returns from WWI addicted to morphine and wounded, his aunt Niska embarks with him on a three-day journey towards their home in the Northern Ontario bush. As Niska paddles along towards their home, the reader slides seamlessly between Xavier’s remembrances of his time at war and Niska’s account of Xavier as a boy. Central to the nove</span>
  <span id="freeText12886043618971387300" style="display:none">A novel that begins at the end, and ends with a beginning. <i>Three Day Road</i> is a stunning debut from one of Canada’s foremost writers, Joseph Boyden. When Xavier Bird returns from WWI addicted to morphine and wounded, his aunt Niska embarks with him on a three-day journey towards their home in the Northern Ontario bush. As Niska paddles along towards their home, the reader slides seamlessly between Xavier’s remembrances of his time at war and Niska’s account of Xavier as a boy. Central to the novel is the uncertain fate of Xavier’s childhood friend and fellow soldier, Elijah Whiskeyjack. As Xavier and Niska make their way closer to their home, so too does a stunning revelation about Elijah and Xavier’s acts creep closer.<br /><br />	From its opening chapter, <i>Three Day Road</i> sets itself up as a book that would put me through the wringer. The novel’s descriptive passages are evocative and powerful. As Elijah and Xavier traipse through the mud, muck, and murder of various WWI killing-fields, I could vividly imagine their struggles and the horrific scope of the world in which they were forced to survive. This is sharply contrasted with the expressive and majestic way in which Boyden paints the wild of Northern Ontario. As alluded to earlier, scenes in which Xavier shoots morphine in the canoe allow for him to slip seamlessly back to the past. The experience was much the same for me as there are never any hiccups as the novel shifts between alternate stories. <br /><br />	The book also resonates with authenticity: the aboriginal themes, thoughts, and practices ring true and never feel forced. Indeed, the three Cree leads are strong, fully developed and interesting characters whose cultural experiences deeply influence their world-view. Elijah and Xavier both fall victim to the torrid world of residential schools, and are enticed up by the promise of glory and adventure that the war will provide. As Niska details her own life, we also see how government has smothered her culture as she clings on to her way of life. Cree mythology also plays a central part in the structure of the novel and the Bird family legacy returns time and again to drive home a powerful theme. <br /><br />	There is so much contained in this novel that it is impossible to do it justice in a 500-word review. The story swept me away, and though it does read slowly, it never felt as if I was putting in work to finish. Xavier’s childhood innocence, his devolution in Europe, and his attempted rehabilitation by his aunt are all told in tandem to make for a staggeringly ambitious first novel. The writing is rich and illustrative, the story neatly tucked into place at its conclusion, and the characters are all compelling. Though a challenging read in content, writing, and emotion, <i>Three Day Road</i> is essential reading for those in Canada and beyond.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12886043618971387300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1255742435" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1255742435" />
    <a title="Warwick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53165-warwick"><img alt="Warwick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510406793p2/53165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1255742435?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Warwick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Warwick" href="/user/show/53165-warwick">Warwick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143056959" href="/book/show/3011111-three-day-road">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=canada">canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=belgium">belgium</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=first-world-war">first-world-war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=ontario">ontario</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1255742435" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16253825424453476883">This seemed like a serendipitous discovery when I stumbled on it in an Ontario bookshop last week. Not literally stumbled – although, come to think of it, there were several piles of books on the floor there which gave browsing something of a parkour flavour. But I had negotiated those hazards successfully. No, I meant stumbled on in the metaphorical sense that I found it by chance. Anyway, can we move on? I have a review to write.<br/><br/>So yes, I hadn't heard of Boyden before, but clearly he's somethi</span>
  <span id="freeText16253825424453476883" style="display:none">This seemed like a serendipitous discovery when I stumbled on it in an Ontario bookshop last week. Not literally stumbled – although, come to think of it, there were several piles of books on the floor there which gave browsing something of a parkour flavour. But I had negotiated those hazards successfully. No, I meant stumbled on in the metaphorical sense that I found it by chance. Anyway, can we move on? I have a review to write.<br><br>So yes, I hadn't heard of Boyden before, but clearly he's something of a literary darling north of the 49th parallel (in Canada – less sure about Kazakhstan) and this novel, his first, begins with what seems to be an entire chapter's worth of adulatory press cuttings to whet your appetite for what follows. Apparently every critic and literary prize in Canada welcomed this one with open arms and legs. By the time you have crawled out of Roman numerals and made it to the start of the story at page 1, you have been primed to be disappointed by anything less than a new <i>Ulysses</i> written on the Stone Tablets of Sinai, with jokes by the ghost of Lenny Bruce.<br><br>It is easy to see why critics got excited about it. This story of two Cree boys from northern Ontario who become snipers in the First World War shines a light on an aspect of 1914–18 that most readers will know little about, and it does so in the uncomplicated, present-tense, flashback-heavy style that is so wildly popular these days.<br><br>Sure enough, there was a lot here I responded to and that filled a gap untouched by my other First World War reading. It is inspired in part by the real-life Ojibwe sniper Francis Pegahmagabow, the most lethal sniper of the war and one of Canada's most decorated (who, as ‘Peggy’, hovers just off-stage at several points in the novel). But the scenes of chaos and misery from the Western Front are never allowed to take over, and they are always interspersed with chapters describing Elijah and Xavier's Cree childhood and family, juxtapositions that offer the reader a range of unusual and productive comparisons that can be made at his or her leisure. This cross-cutting between industrialised slaughter in Europe and the very different ritualised violence of ‘native’ communities reminded me of what Pat Barker attempted with Melanesian islanders in <i>The Ghost Road</i>, though here the conceit is built much more fundamentally into the book's structure.<br><br>This is one of those books that goes for full-on immersive storytelling: it is all about spending plenty of time with these characters, seeing the trenches and the carnage of Ypres and Passchendaele through their eyes, learning, through Xavier's medicine-woman aunt, about how the boys ended up in this place so far away from home.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1429980542i/14656112._SY540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Perhaps the overriding motif is the <i>windigo</i>, that figure of Algonquian mythology associated with cannibalism and insanity. Just as First Nations communities sometimes suffered outbreaks of internal violence that saw people turning in desperation to eating human flesh, so too (we are encouraged to consider) have developed nations in 1914 begun to cannibalise their own population through what seems to be nothing other than collective madness.<br><br><blockquote>I realized then that sadness was at the heart of the <i>windigo</i>, a sadness so pure that it shrivelled the human heart and let something else grow in its place. To know that you have desecrated the ones you love, that you have done something so damning out of a greed for life that you have been exiled from your people forever is a hard meal to swallow, much harder to swallow than that first bite of human flesh.</blockquote><br><br>Much as I enjoyed the story and the general idea, I must admit there was something about the prose style that stopped me from ever loving this book the way I'm sure many others will love it. The prose isn't bad – it just doesn't display much intelligence or wit; there's a kind of flat, undemonstrative quality to it that, perhaps, is appropriate given its narrators but that left me slightly cold. I couldn't shake off a vague sense of <i>Creative Writing courses</i>, reinforced not only by the present-tense narration but by the alternating narrators in different chapters, and the metaphors that tried, I thought, a little too hard to show off their cultural background (‘His skin is the colour of cedar ash in the setting sun’). There is also a certain amount of ‘magic Indian’ stuff going on – a face-value acceptance of some of the Cree mythology and ritual – that sits very uneasily with me.<br><br>Still, this is a book I'd recommend. If you want to know more about Canada's involvement in the war, and First Nations participation in particular, it's a brilliant introduction – and you'd have to be a hard-hearted reader indeed to resist the melodrama of violence and insanity that Boyden skilfully builds up for his climax.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16253825424453476883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1255742435">

<div id="review_1175019541" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1175019541" />
    <a title="Howard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32041201-howard"><img alt="Howard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587918150p2/32041201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1175019541?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Howard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Howard" href="/user/show/32041201-howard">Howard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143037071" href="/book/show/666867.Three_Day_Road">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Howard by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=canada">canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=world-war-l">world-war-l</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1175019541" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5476743345772252493">The gold standard for novels about combat in World War I has always been <b>All Quiet on the Western Front</b> by Erich Maria Remarque, first published in 1929. I first read it many years ago and have since re-read it a couple of times.<br/><br/>There are a number of fabulous goodreads reviews of this classic novel, reviews by Ted, Kemper, Larry Bassett, and Diane Barnes. If you haven't read the book, you should read these reviews and then you may want to. <br/><br/>But I also wish to express my gratitude to several goodr</span>
  <span id="freeText5476743345772252493" style="display:none">The gold standard for novels about combat in World War I has always been <b>All Quiet on the Western Front</b> by Erich Maria Remarque, first published in 1929. I first read it many years ago and have since re-read it a couple of times.<br /><br />There are a number of fabulous goodreads reviews of this classic novel, reviews by Ted, Kemper, Larry Bassett, and Diane Barnes. If you haven't read the book, you should read these reviews and then you may want to. <br /><br />But I also wish to express my gratitude to several goodreads friends, who, through their outstanding reviews, led me to two WWI novels of which I was not aware.<br /><br /><b>Fear: A Novel of World War I</b> by Gabriel Chevalier was originally published in 1930. It was the fascinating reviews of Mike and Sue that led me to a book that I was not aware of, but should have been. It deserves to have the kind of recognition and praise that is accorded <b>All Quiet on the Western Front</b>. It is that good.<br /><br />And so is <b>Three Day Road</b> by Joseph Boyden, published in 2005. Thanks to the great (I'm running out of superlatives) reviews of this book by Michael and Tony. I might not have found it otherwise.<br /><br />The interesting thing for me about the three books is that the authors are writing from three perspectives in that the first is German, the second is French, and the third is Canadian. And yet, the stories they tell about young men from three different nations fighting in the trenches in France are almost interchangeable.<br /><br />The leadership, civilian and military, might have differed in the three countries, but the soldiers who did the fighting were much more alike than they differed.<br /><br />There is a moral there.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5476743345772252493" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143037071" href="/book/show/666867.Three_Day_Road">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14457130-whitney-atkinson?shelf=for-class">for-class</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2124444773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12333568207745081249">edit: it’s been about a month since i’ve read this and i’m increasing my rating to 5 stars. the plot of this book and the main character’s thoughts have just stuck with me so much, and after finishing an 8 page paper about this book it just made me fully comprehend how genius it all flows together. highly recommend.<br/><br/>4.5 Stars.<br/><br/>My war literature professor is just out here trying to make me cry, huh? <br/><br/>This book was beautiful. It's gruesome and explicit, and at times highly uncomfortable, but it's not</span>
  <span id="freeText12333568207745081249" style="display:none">edit: it’s been about a month since i’ve read this and i’m increasing my rating to 5 stars. the plot of this book and the main character’s thoughts have just stuck with me so much, and after finishing an 8 page paper about this book it just made me fully comprehend how genius it all flows together. highly recommend.<br /><br />4.5 Stars.<br /><br />My war literature professor is just out here trying to make me cry, huh? <br /><br />This book was beautiful. It's gruesome and explicit, and at times highly uncomfortable, but it's not just a book about raw killing with no message. The main character has such a fleshed out backstory and inner conflict and I was rooting for him throughout the entire book and just had so much empathy for him even though we're two completely different ethnicities and I've never had to be in a war. I'm usually not a fan of a book being told through flashbacks, but this integrated it nicely and kept me interested enough in knowing what was going on in the past so that they end up where they are. <br /><br />It's a tragic book, but I totally guessed the ending. The reason I took off half a star is because I think I wanted more finality in the ending, and there's still a few things I have questions about that went under-explained. Also, there were so many different flashbacks that sometimes the locations and experiences got muddled since all the trenches were very similar. Also, almost every character in this that I liked died, so it's fINE.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12333568207745081249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_15168507" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15168507" />
    <a title="Malcolm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/783966-malcolm"><img alt="Malcolm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1200369526p2/783966.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15168507?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Malcolm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Malcolm" href="/user/show/783966-malcolm">Malcolm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670063628" href="/book/show/1195395.Three_Day_Road">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15168507" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5325569685164962335">Book Review: Three Day Road<br/> Joseph Boyden<br/> Viking Canada Penguin 2005<br/> ISBN 0-670-06362-2<br/>	Once in a long while one reads a book that you cannot put down and the overall beauty of it leaves one gasping. Three Day Road, is such a book. It tells the story of Two Cree young men who find themselves in WW I fighting in the trenches of France as snipers using their hunting and shooting skills they learned in the bush growing up near James Bay.<br/><br/>	The story begins with the protagonist, Xavier Bird, has retur</span>
  <span id="freeText5325569685164962335" style="display:none">Book Review: Three Day Road<br /> Joseph Boyden<br /> Viking Canada Penguin 2005<br /> ISBN 0-670-06362-2<br />	Once in a long while one reads a book that you cannot put down and the overall beauty of it leaves one gasping. Three Day Road, is such a book. It tells the story of Two Cree young men who find themselves in WW I fighting in the trenches of France as snipers using their hunting and shooting skills they learned in the bush growing up near James Bay.<br /><br />	The story begins with the protagonist, Xavier Bird, has returned from the war minus his leg and addicted to morphine. Aunt Niska, Xavier’s only living relative has paddled five days against the river current to bring him home. Niska is a Cree woman who has lived in traditional ways alone in the bush much of her life except for a brief stay in a residential school as a child. The story takes place during their three day return to James Bay. Flashbacks to the battlefields of France, vividly told, and to each of their childhoods occur during times when Xavier is asleep, or drugged by the morphine that controls the phantom pain in his leg. We learn Xavier is on borrowed time as his morphine cache is nearly gone. The title Three Day Road has a truly layered meaning in the story.<br /><br />	We learn a lot about the traditional native way of life as well as the isolation natives must endure in the world of the wemistikoshiw (the Cree word for white man). <br />We gain a certain insight into a residential school where Cree children are taken from their homes and fat Nuns abuse little boys and punish them severely if they speak in native tongues. <br /><br />	The historically accurate description of life in the trenches in France is excellent – on par with the likes of Pierre Berton’s Vimy.<br /><br />	Boyden’s descriptive and narrative skill is terrific and a lot is included in the books 350 pages. The book is a Writers Trust Award winner and one can easily see why.<br /><br /> I give it five stars (but would give six if I could)<br /><br /> Malcolm Watts BA MSW<br /> Novelist Reflections from Shadow<br /> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.authorsden.com/malcolmwatts">www.authorsden.com/malcolmwatts</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5325569685164962335" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30136851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2408386817522310591">5 stars<br/><br/>I'm not sure I've read any other books inspired by the First World War, but I am sure Joseph's story is different than anything else ever written. <br/><br/>Agreed, I was slow to get TO it (heard him read first chapter in Whistler 2007), and to really get into it, but oh -- when I did, I couldn't turn the pages fast enough!<br/><br/>I read this, for the most part, on the beach while vacationing in Mexico. I couldn't help but react out loud: his battle scenes, e.g., are so vivid, chilling. But saying that sim</span>
  <span id="freeText2408386817522310591" style="display:none">5 stars<br /><br />I'm not sure I've read any other books inspired by the First World War, but I am sure Joseph's story is different than anything else ever written. <br /><br />Agreed, I was slow to get TO it (heard him read first chapter in Whistler 2007), and to really get into it, but oh -- when I did, I couldn't turn the pages fast enough!<br /><br />I read this, for the most part, on the beach while vacationing in Mexico. I couldn't help but react out loud: his battle scenes, e.g., are so vivid, chilling. But saying that simplifies what is really a gripping, epic tale; storytelling at its best.<br /><br />It won the 2005 McNally Robinson Aboriginal Book of the Year; was shortlisted for the Governor General's Literary Award, and went on to become a #1 National Bestseller.<br /><br />I don't want to spoil the story, so I will simply say:"highly recommended". But, if you plan to read Through Black Spruce (Winner of the ScotiaGiller Prize), hold off, and read this book first. "You'll be glad you did." :)<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2408386817522310591" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1711189558" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1711189558" />
    <a title="Maxwell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell"><img alt="Maxwell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539318702p2/1381728.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1711189558?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maxwell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maxwell" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell">Maxwell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143037071" href="/book/show/666867.Three_Day_Road">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1381728-maxwell?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1711189558" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11108010550484602234">I'm a huge fan of Boyden's work. I was really impressed by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17661831.The_Orenda' title='The Orenda by Joseph Boyden' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Orenda</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3479967.Through_Black_Spruce' title='Through Black Spruce by Joseph Boyden' rel='nofollow noopener'> Through Black Spruce</a>, and loved his stories in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4578640.Born_with_a_Tooth' title='Born with a Tooth by Joseph Boyden' rel='nofollow noopener'> Born with a Tooth</a>. When I finally got around to his debut novel, I think it proved to be a bit underwhelming.<br/><br/>Stylistically, Boyden is as strong as ever in this one; his writing is sharp, observant, and transportive. The ways in which he weaves together both Niska and Xavier's stories is excellent. And the tension that builds throughout between Elijah and Xavier is palpable.</span>
  <span id="freeText11108010550484602234" style="display:none">I'm a huge fan of Boyden's work. I was really impressed by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17661831.The_Orenda" title="The Orenda by Joseph Boyden" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Orenda</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3479967.Through_Black_Spruce" title="Through Black Spruce by Joseph Boyden" rel="nofollow noopener"> Through Black Spruce</a>, and loved his stories in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4578640.Born_with_a_Tooth" title="Born with a Tooth by Joseph Boyden" rel="nofollow noopener"> Born with a Tooth</a>. When I finally got around to his debut novel, I think it proved to be a bit underwhelming.<br /><br />Stylistically, Boyden is as strong as ever in this one; his writing is sharp, observant, and transportive. The ways in which he weaves together both Niska and Xavier's stories is excellent. And the tension that builds throughout between Elijah and Xavier is palpable. I thought all the elements where there for a fantastic story, but it just didn't quite reach its full potential. Maybe it's one of those 'it's not you, it's me' stories? Admittedly I'm not the biggest fan of WWI narratives. I thought many of the battle sequences and depictions of the European frontlines were redundant and did little to move the story forward. I was most fascinated hearing about Xavier's childhood and Niska's recollection of the boys' friendship.<br /><br />Nonetheless, I love Boyden's ability to write from multiple perspectives—as he does in most of his books—and create vivid settings. If only it hadn't dragged so much in the middle this would've been a better book for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11108010550484602234" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_380136000" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/380136000" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086118-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567611029p2/4086118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/380136000?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670034314" href="/book/show/1542683.Three_Day_Road">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=canada">canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=racism">racism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=survival">survival</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=native-american">native-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=world-war-1">world-war-1</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=ontario">ontario</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer380136000" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9980223268952739529">I love Erdrich's blurb for the book: "a devastatingly truthful work of fiction, and a masterful account of hell and healing. This is a grave, grand, and passionate book." This is the story of a three-day canoe journey home for Cree Indian Xavier Bird, who arrives by train in northern Ontario severely damaged from his experience as an infantry soldier in World War 1. He has lost a leg and is addicted to morphine. He is accompanied by his only family member, his aunt Niska, a medicine woman who ra</span>
  <span id="freeText9980223268952739529" style="display:none">I love Erdrich's blurb for the book: "a devastatingly truthful work of fiction, and a masterful account of hell and healing. This is a grave, grand, and passionate book." This is the story of a three-day canoe journey home for Cree Indian Xavier Bird, who arrives by train in northern Ontario severely damaged from his experience as an infantry soldier in World War 1. He has lost a leg and is addicted to morphine. He is accompanied by his only family member, his aunt Niska, a medicine woman who raised him in remote bush after liberating him at age 5 from a residential school. A pervasive mystery for Niska is the fate of Xavier's friend and effective brother Elijah, whom she helped raise from an older age and went off to war with Xavier. The narrative skillfully alternates between Xavier's upbringing and his experience in war with Elijah, where they became snipers in the trench warfare in France and Belgium. The account of the common foot soldier's perspective on the fighting is to me on a par with "All Quiet on the Western Front". At the same time, the novel provides a timeless fresh vision of alienation associated the human failure of 20th century civilization through the lens of Native American culture. The transformation of his friend Elijah into an amoral killing machine epitomizes the corruption, insanity, and evils of the whole enterprise, which represents a big challenge for Niska to help Xavier recover from.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9980223268952739529" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Connie G" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g"><img alt="Connie G" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307502493p2/5364077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Connie G" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Connie G" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g">Connie G</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=canada">canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=world-war-i">world-war-i</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=native-american-or-canadian">native-american-or-canadian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer488513963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4705933692515672748">Xavier Bird struggles on crutches as he descends from the train in northern Canada in 1919. He is in terrible pain and addicted to morphine since a war injury resulted in the amputation of his leg. The relentless horror in the trenches of the Western Front have also taken their toll emotionally. His aunt Niska, an Oji-Cree medicine woman, takes Kavier home in a three day journey in her canoe.<br/><br/>The Canadian government had a forced residential education program, attempting to eradicate the First Nat</span>
  <span id="freeText4705933692515672748" style="display:none">Xavier Bird struggles on crutches as he descends from the train in northern Canada in 1919. He is in terrible pain and addicted to morphine since a war injury resulted in the amputation of his leg. The relentless horror in the trenches of the Western Front have also taken their toll emotionally. His aunt Niska, an Oji-Cree medicine woman, takes Kavier home in a three day journey in her canoe.<br /><br />The Canadian government had a forced residential education program, attempting to eradicate the First Nation's culture, but Niska had escaped into the bush as a teenager. She later rescued her nephew Xavier, and his friend Elijah from the harsh environment of the school. They were taught the skills of tracking and hunting game, and both became skilled marksmen. The two young men volunteered for service in the Canadian Army, mostly because Elijah had a taste for adventure. Their skill with rifles is soon recognized, and they become an expert sniper and scout team. The taking of lives has a different cumulative effect on each of the two friends. While one becomes repelled by the bloodshed, the other turns into an obsessive killer.<br /><br />The First Nation has a long tradition of storytelling. As Niska paddles her canoe, she tells Xavier stories from her youth to distract him from his pain. In flashbacks, Xavier relives the years on the Western Front in morphine-induced memories. Xavier's supply of morphine is running low. Will Niska be able to heal her troubled, broken nephew?<br /><br />The Canadian Second Division fought bravely in Flanders, the Nord-Pas-de-Calais, and the Somme. Joseph Boyden has melded history, First Nation culture, and good storytelling to create an absorbing tale. The author's family background is part Ojibwa, and includes military men. Boyden's World War I story pulled me down into the trenches, and into the sniper's nest. I'm looking forward to the next two books in the trilogy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4705933692515672748" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9719156018155444037">This is a phenomenal and haunting book. Loved the story despite most of it being set in the WWI. Maybe because it not the traditional stories told of WWI. The characters are deep and complex. So powerfully written that it makes you stop and wonder what hell is going on with society. It's like a stab to the heart. It will make you very uncomfortable. <br/><br/>The story follows Xavier Bird, a young First Nations Canadian, journey through leaving the bush, enlisting in and serving as a sniper in WWI, then t</span>
  <span id="freeText9719156018155444037" style="display:none">This is a phenomenal and haunting book. Loved the story despite most of it being set in the WWI. Maybe because it not the traditional stories told of WWI. The characters are deep and complex. So powerfully written that it makes you stop and wonder what hell is going on with society. It's like a stab to the heart. It will make you very uncomfortable. <br /><br />The story follows Xavier Bird, a young First Nations Canadian, journey through leaving the bush, enlisting in and serving as a sniper in WWI, then through his the healing process of not only his body but his mind and spirit by returning to his home in the bush. It also of his aunt, Niska, who as been able to evade the assimilation into residential schools and the destruction of her traditional knowledge and spared the young Xavier Bird from the same.<br /><br />It's very Canadiana. Joseph Boyden has an amazing ability of being able to share stories of Native First Nation Canadians experiences in a honest and true form. The school children of North America, and particularly of Canada, should be reading this in their English classes. </span>

<div id="review_237095689" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/237095689" />
    <a title="Jennifer (aka EM)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em"><img alt="Jennifer (aka EM)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510513814p2/1393336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/237095689?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer (aka EM)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer (aka EM)" href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em">Jennifer (aka EM)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1393336-jennifer-aka-em?shelf=war-is-hell">war-is-hell</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1393336-jennifer-aka-em?shelf=maple-flavoured">maple-flavoured</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer237095689" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4539148880591484280">This is two of my favourite reads: a "futility-of-war" novel by a Native Canadian writer, and with a unique Native Canadian angle.<br/><br/>Xavier and Elijah are Ojibwe-Cree from "the North Country" (which in this case means James Bay area) who sign up for WW I, and - because of their hunting prowess - make for excellent warriors. Niska - X's auntie - welcomes a deeply changed X home, and does what she can to help X cope with all he has seen, suffered and lost.<br/><br/>The novel is about killing and healing and in</span>
  <span id="freeText4539148880591484280" style="display:none">This is two of my favourite reads: a "futility-of-war" novel by a Native Canadian writer, and with a unique Native Canadian angle.<br /><br />Xavier and Elijah are Ojibwe-Cree from "the North Country" (which in this case means James Bay area) who sign up for WW I, and - because of their hunting prowess - make for excellent warriors. Niska - X's auntie - welcomes a deeply changed X home, and does what she can to help X cope with all he has seen, suffered and lost.<br /><br />The novel is about killing and healing and incredible, profound, spirit-driven love. Of the boys for each other; of Niska for her would-be sons, and for X in particular. Love and bonds that are forged in one kind of trauma and tested in another kind of hell. Of a way of life that is lost to all kinds of <i>wemistikishiw</i> slaughter and madness; of transition between one way of life and another, physical, cultural, spiritual.<br /><br />Though I am not a masochist, both types of novels - the war novel, the Native Canadian novel - cause great pain and therefore they often feel cathartic, cleansing in some way. But more: like an atonement for my privileged whiteness and the luck of the draw time and place-wise. <br /><br />This idea of privilege - and how it factors into reading choices - is an interesting one to me these days (as I look at my bookshelves stacked to the brim with female authors; goodreads having made me more acutely aware of gender aspects of writing and reading). <br /><br />Boyden, with his sensitivity; his writing that contains such depth of raw emotion; his male-femaleness/female-maleness (as Woolf would say) - has pride of place next to Erdrich, Thomas King and Richard Wagamese; and novels like Marlantes' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6411016.Matterhorn_A_Novel_of_the_Vietnam_War" title="Matterhorn A Novel of the Vietnam War by Karl Marlantes" rel="nofollow noopener">Matterhorn</a>, Vonnegut's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4981.Slaughterhouse_Five" title="Slaughterhouse-Five by Kurt Vonnegut" rel="nofollow noopener">Slaughterhouse Five</a>, Findley's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29898.The_Wars" title="The Wars by Timothy Findley" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wars</a> and Wright's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/297334.Meditations_in_Green" title="Meditations in Green by Stephen Wright" rel="nofollow noopener">Meditations In Green</a>.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4539148880591484280" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1569218929" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1569218929" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31384949-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584889764p2/31384949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1569218929?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/31384949-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753820810" href="/book/show/2236145.Three_Day_Road">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=wwi">wwi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=canada">canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1569218929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3381432769112745724">The time setting is 1919, and at a train station in Northern Ontario a Native Canadian woman, Niska, meets her nephew, Xavier Bird, on his return from active service in WWI. Xavier returns from the war a broken man in every sense. One leg has been amputated below the knee, he is addicted to morphine, and he is suffering from severe post traumatic stress. The two make a three day canoe journey back to Niska's camp, and during the journey the story of Xavier's wartime experiences is told in flashb</span>
  <span id="freeText3381432769112745724" style="display:none">The time setting is 1919, and at a train station in Northern Ontario a Native Canadian woman, Niska, meets her nephew, Xavier Bird, on his return from active service in WWI. Xavier returns from the war a broken man in every sense. One leg has been amputated below the knee, he is addicted to morphine, and he is suffering from severe post traumatic stress. The two make a three day canoe journey back to Niska's camp, and during the journey the story of Xavier's wartime experiences is told in flashback style, interspersed with the story of Niska's life and Xavier's pre-war childhood. The novel's central relationship is between Xavier and his childhood friend Elijah Whiskeyjack, who is also his comrade in the trenches, but who undergoes a progressive brutalisation as the war itself progresses.<br /><br />The author has written another novel "Through Black Spruce", featuring Xavier's children and grandchildren, which I had previously read and which I thought was excellent. That gave me high expectations of "Three Day Road" which I would have to say were not fully realised. There are plenty of plus points. The quality of writing is good, and the novel has a strong culture clash theme as well as the inherent pathos of a WWI setting. In some ways it also challenged my own sense of morality, and it is no bad thing when a novel gets you thinking in that way. At the same time, I never found myself emotionally engaged with either the main characters or the plot as a whole. It probably didn't help that from early chapters I started to anticipate the broad outline of the plot, and it did turn out much as I had thought. For me, the plot also incorporated too much magic and mysticism. I have no overriding objections to including those kind of elements in a novel, but in this one too much was resolved simply by the summoning or exorcism of spirits, a plot device I found very unconvincing.<br /><br />Glancing at the other reviews, my view seems to be a minority one, and most other people have rated this much more highly. I can only say what my honest reaction was.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3381432769112745724" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_53316112" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53316112" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/53316112?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753820810" href="/book/show/2236145.Three_Day_Road">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=kirkus">kirkus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=text-checked">text-checked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=canada">canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2012-read">2012-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=ww1">ww1</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53316112" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14203118947277467467">This book is loosely based on the famed Native Canadian WW1 sniper Francis Pegahmagabow. It is about WW1 trench warfare; it is about the role Native Canadians played in this warfare and it is about mystical Cree beliefs. I think this book goes a step deeper. It is about warfare in general and also about taking another person’s life <u>outside of the war setting</u>. I am left very troubled by the ending. I have more questions after reading this book than before. Am I a pacifist? Am I against all wars? </span>
  <span id="freeText14203118947277467467" style="display:none">This book is loosely based on the famed Native Canadian WW1 sniper Francis Pegahmagabow. It is about WW1 trench warfare; it is about the role Native Canadians played in this warfare and it is about mystical Cree beliefs. I think this book goes a step deeper. It is about warfare in general and also about taking another person’s life <u>outside of the war setting</u>. I am left very troubled by the ending. I have more questions after reading this book than before. Am I a pacifist? Am I against all wars? This book puts you in the trenches and you see what it is <u>really </u> like there, well how it was fighting in France during WW1. You learn more than just about death and suffering and pain, but also grit and filth and morphine and human bonding. But the real question is: when does killing go over the edge? Isn’t a soldier supposed to kill as many as possible? When is it right? When does it all of a sudden become wrong? It is right here that this book upsets me. I am left with unresolved questions, and, more specifically, do I agree with what the author is suggesting? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Should Xavier have killed Elijah and taken his identity papers? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Finally, the book is also very much about healing. How does one attain physical, emotional and spiritual healing after living through such war horrors? Here again I have problems with the book. The solutions proffered are too simplistic. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ The Cree <i>matatosowin</i> ritual is not a solution I can wrap my head around. It is too simplistic! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Remember, for me, a three star book is worth reading!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14203118947277467467" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2422256375" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2422256375" />
    <a title="Susan&#39;s Reviews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19736308-susan-s-reviews"><img alt="Susan&#39;s Reviews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507651790p2/19736308.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2422256375?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan&#39;s Reviews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan&#39;s Reviews" href="/user/show/19736308-susan-s-reviews">Susan&#39;s Reviews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2422256375" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14768187824047375095">Truly heroic characters in terrible circumstances. I've read most of this author's books. Couldn't finish the Orenda - waaaaay too violent for me, but the fair chunk of it that I did read was very well written. Through Black Spruce was my favourite of all of Boyden's books.</span>

          </span>
        </div>
